  //<![CDATA[
    function loadScript(url, callback) {
      var script = document.createElement("script");
      script.type = "text/javascript";
    
      if (script.readyState) {  //Internet Explorer
          script.onreadystatechange = function() {
            if (script.readyState == "loaded" ||
                    script.readyState == "complete") {
              script.onreadystatechange = null;
              callback();
            }
          };
      } else {  //Other browsers
        script.onload = function() {
          callback();
        };
      }
    
      script.src = url;
      document.getElementsByTagName("head")[0].appendChild(script);
    }

      AppleID.auth.init({
        clientId : 'com.goodreads.app',

<div id="descriptionContainer">
  
      <div id="description" class="readable stacked" style="right:0">
        
<span id="freeTextContainer11410573172721486358">Elric of Melniboné is a requisite title in the hard fantasy canon, a book no fantasy fan should leave unread. Author Michael Moorcock, already a major player in science fiction, cemented his position in the fantasy pantheon with the five-book Elric saga, of which Elric of Melniboné is the first installment. The book's namesake, the brooding albino emperor of the dying nati</span>
  <span id="freeText11410573172721486358" style="display:none">Elric of Melniboné is a requisite title in the hard fantasy canon, a book no fantasy fan should leave unread. Author Michael Moorcock, already a major player in science fiction, cemented his position in the fantasy pantheon with the five-book Elric saga, of which Elric of Melniboné is the first installment. The book's namesake, the brooding albino emperor of the dying nation of Melniboné, is a sort of Superman for Goths, truly an archetype of the genre.<br /><br />The youthful Elric is a cynical and melancholy king, heir to a nation whose 100,000-year rule of the world ended less than 500 years hence. More interested in brooding contemplation than holding the throne, Elric is a reluctant ruler, but he also realizes that no other worthy successor exists and the survival of his once-powerful, decadent nation depends on him alone. Elric's nefarious, brutish cousin Yrkoon has no patience for his physically weak kinsman, and he plots constantly to seize Elric's throne, usually over his dead body. Elric of Melniboné follows Yrkoon's scheming, reaching its climax in a battle between Elric and Yrkoon with the demonic runeblades Stormbringer and Mournblade. In this battle, Elric gains control of the soul-stealing Stormbringer, an event that proves pivotal to the Elric saga. --Paul Hughes</span>
  <a data-text-id="11410573172721486358" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

      </div>

<div id="review_245324277" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/245324277" />
    <a title="J.G. Keely" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/84023-j-g-keely"><img alt="J.G. Keely" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1274333636p2/84023.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/245324277?book_show_action=true">Dec 10, 2011</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="J.G. Keely" class="user" itemprop="url" name="J.G. Keely" href="/user/show/84023-j-g-keely">J.G. Keely</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/84023-j-g-keely?shelf=uk-and-ireland">uk-and-ireland</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/84023-j-g-keely?shelf=reviewed">reviewed</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/84023-j-g-keely?shelf=fantasy">fantasy</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/84023-j-g-keely?shelf=sword-and-sorcery">sword-and-sorcery</a>
          </div>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer245324277" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer9730624094892188783">I have spent a long time searching for a modern fantastical epic which is worth reading. It seems like there should be one, out there, somewhere. I have so enjoyed the <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/58776632' rel='nofollow noopener'>battlefields of Troy</a>, the <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1429679' rel='nofollow noopener'>dank cavern</a> of Grendel's dam, Dido's lament, Ovid's hundred <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1637865' rel='nofollow noopener'>wild-spun tales</a>, <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/65207218' rel='nofollow noopener'>perfidious Odysseus</a>, the <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1455037' rel='nofollow noopener'>madness</a> of Orlando, Satan's <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1194644' rel='nofollow noopener'>twisted rhetoric</a>, and <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/61980644' rel='nofollow noopener'>Gilgamesh's sea-voyage</a> to the forgotten lands of death. And so I seek some modern author to reinvent these tales with some sense of scholarship, poetry, c</span>
  <span id="freeText9730624094892188783" style="display:none">I have spent a long time searching for a modern fantastical epic which is worth reading. It seems like there should be one, out there, somewhere. I have so enjoyed the <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/58776632" rel="nofollow noopener">battlefields of Troy</a>, the <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1429679" rel="nofollow noopener">dank cavern</a> of Grendel's dam, Dido's lament, Ovid's hundred <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1637865" rel="nofollow noopener">wild-spun tales</a>, <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/65207218" rel="nofollow noopener">perfidious Odysseus</a>, the <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1455037" rel="nofollow noopener">madness</a> of Orlando, Satan's <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1194644" rel="nofollow noopener">twisted rhetoric</a>, and <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/61980644" rel="nofollow noopener">Gilgamesh's sea-voyage</a> to the forgotten lands of death. And so I seek some modern author to reinvent these tales with some sense of scholarship, poetry, character, and adventure.<br /><br />There are many great modern fantasies, but the epic subgenre lacks luster. In reading the offerings--<a href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1459299" rel="nofollow noopener">Martin</a>, <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1195401" rel="nofollow noopener">Jordan</a>, <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1195463" rel="nofollow noopener">Goodkind</a>, <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1195412" rel="nofollow noopener">Paolini</a>, even much-lauded <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/6736950" rel="nofollow noopener">Wolfe</a>--I have found them all wanting. They are all flawed in the same ways: their protagonists are dull caricatures of some universal 'badass' ideal, plot conflicts are glossed-over with magic or convenient deaths, the magic itself is not a mysterious force but a familiar tool, and women are made secondary or worse (though the authors often talk about how women are strong and independent, the women never actually act that way).<br /><br />But then, they are all acolytes of <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/57631270" rel="nofollow noopener">Old Tolkien</a>, who is as stodgy, unromantic, and methodical as a fantasist can be (without being <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/163172902" rel="nofollow noopener">C.S. Lewis</a>). Though I respect Tolkien's work as a well-researched literary exercise, it is hard to forgive him for making it acceptable to write fantasy which is so dull, aimless, and self-absorbed. It is unfortunate that so many people think that fantasy began with Tolkien, because that is a great falsehood, and anyone who believes it does not really know fantasy at all. It nearly died with him.<br /><br />Yet there are many who do think he started it. They like to comment on reviews, especially reviews of their favorite books--especially <i>negative</i> reviews of their favorite books--which have, lamentably, become a specialty of mine. And often, they end up asking me "Well, what fantasy do <i>you</i> like?" There are many I could name, numerous favorites which have shocked and overawed me, which have shaken me to my core, which have shown me worlds and magic I dared not dream. But none of them are epics.<br /><br />I could mention Susanna Clarke's <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1195173" rel="nofollow noopener">Jonathan Strange and Mr. Norrell</a>, a powerfully self-possessed work and one of the only fantasies of the past twenty years that I consider worth reading--the other is China Mieville's <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1194650" rel="nofollow noopener">Perdido Street Station</a>--but these are a Victorian alternate history continuation of the British Fairy Tale tradition and a New Weird Urban Fantasy, respectively. I could mention Mervyn Peake's <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1203852" rel="nofollow noopener">Titus books</a>, which so powerfully inhabit my five-star rating that Mieville and Clarke must be relegated to four--but this is a work whose fantastical nature would probably not even be apparent to most fantasy enthusiasts.<br /><br />Alas they are not good counter-examples. I can (and do) mention Robert E. Howard's <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/133988083" rel="nofollow noopener">Conan</a>, and Fritz Leiber's <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/35503587" rel="nofollow noopener">Lankhmar series</a>, but these are fast-paced adventure stories, and though their worlds may be vast, mysterious, and grand, the stories themselves lack the hyperopic arc at the heart of an epic work.<br /><br />But there have been many suggestions, many readers who have come to my aid, and who have named authors I might look to next, in my quest: Guy Gavriel Kay, Ursula K. LeGuin, Jack Vance, Poul Anderson, Jeff VanderMeer, Michael De Larrabietti, John M. Harrison, Scott Lynch, Patricia McKillip, and John Crowley (Brandon Sanderson and Patrick Rothfuss have been both suggested and sneered at). It is my hope that, somewhere amongst them, I will find the exemplary epic fantasy I am looking for--but I haven't found it in Moorcock.<br /><br />Moorcock is good, he has scope, depth, complexity, and long, twisting plots, but at their core, his stories are modern, metaphysical, and subversive. They are light and lilting, ironical and wry--too quick and twisting to be 'epic'. The characters are introspective and self-aware, and it is clear that it is they, and not the world, who will be at the forefront.<br /><br />It is all so thoroughly modern, so reinvented, full of sprightly ideas and metaphysical brooding. But it is decidedly <i>not</i> modern in the accidental, self-defeating ways of all those pretenders to the 'epic' title. The characters are not merely the male-fantasy counterpart of a bodice ripper, with modern, familiar minds dressed thinly in Medieval costume. The world is not simply our world with an overlay of castles--dragons for jet fighters, spells for guns, with modern politics and sensibilities.<br /><br />No, Moorcock's world and characters are alien and fantastical, but Moorcock does not achieve this by ripping them whole-cloth from history, but by extrapolating them from modern philosophical ideas. Fantasy stories have always been full of dreamscapes, of impossible places for the reader to inhabit. These places draw us in, somehow we recognize them, like our own dreams, because of what they represent.<br /><br />Anthropomorphism is the human tendency to see people where there are none: to see smiling faces in wood grain, to assign complex emotional motivations to cats, and to curse at the storm that breaks our window. The 'Other World' of British Fairy Tales is based on the latter: the assigning of our luck--good and bad--to capricious spirits. The world of fairy has rules (as do storms), but those rules are mostly a mystery to man.<br /><br />But Moorcock's world personifies the ideas of Kant and Nietzsche: his 'Other Worlds' (called 'Planes') are those of the human mind: they are places of morality, like heaven and hell, except he has updated the concept to existential morality. There is Chaos, and there is Law; Chaos is the selfish urge, Law the communal urge, and he arrays his magic, spirits, and dreamscapes along this axis.<br /><br />Like Milton, he has infused his epic with the latest thoughts and notions, updating it for the modern age. Also like Milton, Moorcock's influence has been felt, far and wide, despite the fact that most people do not recognize it.<br /><br />The Dungeons &amp; Dragons game prominently used his Law/Chaos dichotomy, among other concepts, and his 'Wheel of Psychic Planes' is an influence on their most audacious and unusual publication, the philosophical 'Steampunk' setting, <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1832989" rel="nofollow noopener">Planescape</a>. And many of these tropes have filtered down into the grab-bag common to the modern voice of fantasy stories.<br /><br />Reading Elric, one will invariably be reminded of a dozen other books and games, as Elric drinks endless potions to maintain his strength and vitality, slaying twisted demons on a plane of fire in search of a rune-sword, dressed in ornate black armor and a dragon-helm. Indeed, the central mythology (and much of the plot) of the Elder Scrolls games--in particular Oblivion--owe a vast debt to Elric and his world, and not simply for the land of 'Elwher'.<br /><br />Clearly, Moorcock's odd vision has been transcribed onto the imaginations of fantasists, but as with those who were inspired by Tolkien, most of his followers have failed to recreate the weight of the original message. Except for a few outliers, like Planescape and Perdido Street Station, most authors have copied the outward appearance of Moorcock's alien world, but were not skilled or knowledgeable enough to take the substance along with the form--the existential ideas, the vital core of his dreamscapes, are most often missing, or at best, faded.<br /><br />But while the ideas and the overall vision are strong--even compared to the ubiquitous attempts to recreate them--there are a number of flaws in Moorcock's presentation. The first and most damaging is a weakness in the voice. Moorcock has a lot to say, but must sometimes resort to explaining his ideas to us. He is not always able to deliver his world and characters through interactions, hints, tone, and actions. He is hardly an inexperienced enough author to explain to us that which is already self-evident, but it is a weakness in his delivery which sometimes takes us out of the flow of the story, so that we must step back from the world and listen to Moorcock talk about it, though he does do his best to veil it with Elric's thoughts.<br /><br />Secondly, it can be difficult to get a strong impression of his characters, they are often difficult to sympathize with or to predict. It isn't that they aren't vivid and active, but that their actions are often based around ideas and concepts--the things Moorcock built his world on--which can create a sense of a top-down world, where the characters are there to fulfill a purpose, to explore various notions and philosophies. <br /><br />The book is certainly not an allegory--there are no easy one-to-one correlations to be made between characters and ideas, but the world does not revolve around personalities--except, perhaps, for Elric's, but his thoughts and motivations are often the most difficult to reconcile. The personalities of all the other characters are, more or less, wholly dependent on him.<br /><br />To some degree, the characters seem to operate on much older fantasy rules: their capricious yet repetitive acts becoming motifs for the larger ideas in the story, not unlike Tolkien's fantasy forefather, <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/10072999" rel="nofollow noopener">E.R. Eddison</a>, whose characters seem half-mad with heroism for its own sake (another candidate for my favorite epic, if I didn't think his beautiful, deliberate archaism might prove too remote for many readers).<br /><br />Part of the reason for this is that Elric's personality and world were created as an exercise, and with an explicit purpose: to portray the anti-Conan. He is sickly, weak, pale, effeminate, sorcerous, erudite, cruel, reluctant, intellectual, and hardly promiscuous. Conan becomes king by his own hand, while Elric begins as emperor and we witness the hardships of his downfall.<br /><br />But this contrariness, while coloring the story, is hardly its center. Moorcock uses it as a springboard--an inspiration to drive him to something greater. It is one more example of the fact that genius is at its best when it has a lofty challenge before it. Moorcock is not interested in making a parody, but in exploring a little-trodden path, operating on the notion that if you start with something familiar and begin to move away from it, you are bound to end up somewhere else.<br /><br />I must also mention an unbelievable incident involving a group of blind soldiers, which put dire strain to credulity. A bit of creative myth or capricious magic could have saved it, but as it stands in the book, it makes little sense.<br /><br />But despite the subtle weaknesses in voice and characterization, Moorcock's idiomatic adventure story is eminently enjoyable. There are few fantasy books I could name which suggest such a playful intellect as this, and though it is not as wildly imaginative as his <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1194611" rel="nofollow noopener">Gloriana</a>, this philosophical exploration disguised as a pulp adventure is a delightful read that never gets bogged-down in indulging its own thoughtfulness.<br /><br /><a href="http://starsbeetlesandfools.blogspot.com/2012/06/suggested-readings-in-fantasy.html" rel="nofollow noopener">My List of Suggested Fantasy Books</a></span>
  <a data-text-id="9730624094892188783" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <span id="freeText8189137713709675343" style="display:none">Elric of FREAKIN’ Melnibone.<br><br>A supersized but colorless everlasting gobstopper of fantasy have-at-you.<br><br>If he were a mongoose, he’d be Elrici-tiki-tavi.<br><br>If he were a professional wrestler (pronounced RASTLER in Memphis) he’d be Elric Flair - Wooooooooooooo!<br><br>If he were an amateur bowler in California and part time brother Shamus he’d be El Ricerino (and only if we were not into the whole brevity thing.)<br><br>If he were anything other than Michael Moorcock’s magnificent Man of Melnibone he’d be a calorie deficient, sorcery and drugs addicted, disabled inbred monarch on a cold seat.<br><br>He is Elric.<br><br>“Conan! What is best in life?”<br><br>“To crush enemy, see him driven before you, to hear the lamentation of the vomen, and to never have to mess with that white guy with the bigass black sword.”<br><br>You’re damn skippy, Conan. <br><br>Elric sprang forth from the Lovecraftian demented mind of English writer Michael Moorcock. Elric of Melnibone started the ball rolling in 1972 and has been showing fantasy writers how its done ever since.<br><br>A MUST read for all fantasy readers.<br><br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1460200883i/18711879._SX540_.jpg" width="400" height="300" alt="description" class="gr-hostedUserImg"></span>

<div id="review_134618548" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/134618548" />
    <a title="Stephen" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1413439-stephen"><img alt="Stephen" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1311616811p2/1413439.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/134618548?book_show_action=true">Dec 11, 2010</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Stephen" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Stephen" href="/user/show/1413439-stephen">Stephen</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1413439-stephen?shelf=hardboiled-and-noir">hardboiled-and-noir</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1413439-stephen?shelf=kings-of-badassia">kings-of-badassia</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1413439-stephen?shelf=audiobook">audiobook</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1413439-stephen?shelf=1970-1979">1970-1979</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1413439-stephen?shelf=fantasy">fantasy</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1413439-stephen?shelf=moorcocks-broken-sword">moorcocks-broken-sword</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1413439-stephen?shelf=sorcerous-swords">sorcerous-swords</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer134618548" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer16719421564694910784">After reading and having my <b>hair blown back</b> by <i>The Swords Trilogy</i> (<a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/399376.The_Knight_of_the_Swords__Corum___1_' title='The Knight of the Swords (Corum, #1) by Michael Moorcock' rel='nofollow noopener'>The Knight of the Swords</a>,<a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/658625.The_Queen_of_the_Swords__Corum___2_' title='The Queen of the Swords (Corum, #2) by Michael Moorcock' rel='nofollow noopener'>The Queen of the Swords</a> and <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/699518.The_King_of_the_Swords__Corum___3_' title='The King of the Swords (Corum, #3) by Michael Moorcock' rel='nofollow noopener'>The King of the Swords</a>), I decided to dive into the adventures of Moorcock's <b>most famous</b> avatar of the <b>Eternal Champion</b>, Elric of Melnibone.<br/><br/>This first installment serves as a nice introduction to the contemplative albino sorcerer, who rules the ancient, powerful land of Melnibone. It was a nice surprise to learn that in the never ending, multi-dimensional cosmic dust up between</span>
  <span id="freeText16719421564694910784" style="display:none">After reading and having my <b>hair blown back</b> by <i>The Swords Trilogy</i> (<a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/399376.The_Knight_of_the_Swords__Corum___1_" title="The Knight of the Swords (Corum, #1) by Michael Moorcock" rel="nofollow noopener">The Knight of the Swords</a>,<a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/658625.The_Queen_of_the_Swords__Corum___2_" title="The Queen of the Swords (Corum, #2) by Michael Moorcock" rel="nofollow noopener">The Queen of the Swords</a> and <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/699518.The_King_of_the_Swords__Corum___3_" title="The King of the Swords (Corum, #3) by Michael Moorcock" rel="nofollow noopener">The King of the Swords</a>), I decided to dive into the adventures of Moorcock's <b>most famous</b> avatar of the <b>Eternal Champion</b>, Elric of Melnibone.<br /><br />This first installment serves as a nice introduction to the contemplative albino sorcerer, who rules the ancient, powerful land of Melnibone. It was a nice surprise to learn that in the never ending, multi-dimensional cosmic dust up between the Lords of Order and the Lords of Chaos, Elric's people, the Melniboneans, are aligned with the bad guys at Chaos and are viewed by the pesky, upstart humans as evil, a perception that has considerable merit. I thought it added a nice shading to Elric's introduction as an anti-hero by showing his as a moral outsider to his own people.   <br /><br />Elric, who assumed the throne upon the death of his father, has always been “uncomfortable” with his people’s darker ways and their allegiance to the Lords of Chaos. While no angel by human standards, his appetites have never run to the less savory customs of his people making him somewhat of a radical among his people. When he tries to introduce reforms to his society, he causes all kinds of pissed off among the "old guard" who view him as weak upstart and troublemaker. <br /><br />This sets up one of the fundamental themes of the book as Elric struggles with his identity and his place in a society in which he does not feel fully at home.<br /><br />As with <i>The Swords Trilogy</i>, Moorcock's writing is lush and lyrical without being long-winded. The prose is also infused with just a hint of melodrama that works very well for this kind of sword and sorcery story.  In addition, the world-building is jam-packed with idea and concept tidbits that are wonderfully and really add flavor to the narrative.  I have become a big fan of Moorcock's style and plan to revisit the world of Elric...often. <br /><br />One of my favorite Moorcockian nuggets from this book was the concept of the “dream couch” that is used by the Melnibonean rulers. On the dream couch, an hour of "real" time can be the equivalent of “ten thousand years or more” of subjective time to the person using it. It is on the dream couch that the Melnibonean rulers (including Elric) learn the arts of sorcery, combat and other skills that would normally take them more than a lifetime to learn. <br /><br />I nearly <b>splooged</b> when I read this and thought the concept was a terrific way to plausibly show (within the context of the fantastic) how a young Elric could have attained such a high degree of <b>badassness</b> and <b>jedi mastered</b> so many talents in such a short time. Moorcock's amazing concept was not only fun to read about, but provided credibility for the rest of the story and I was completely sucked in from there. I've always thought that good fantasy should make us suspend our disbelief, not our intelligence. This is GOOD FANTASY. <br /><br />Bottom-line, this is another high quality sword and sorcery effort by one of the best to ever write in that genre. 4.0 stars. HIGHLY RECOMMENDED. <br /><br />P.S. As a final note, I want to point out that I listened to the audio version of this book read by Jeffrey West (with an introduction by the author) and the quality of the reading was really superb. It included music in the background during the entire reading which could have been a big distraction but in this case added significantly to the “ambiance” of the story. I highly recommend it if you have the chance to check it out. <br /><br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="16719421564694910784" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1290974052" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1290974052" />
    <a title="Markus" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/26681101-markus"><img alt="Markus" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1421952301p2/26681101.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1290974052?book_show_action=true">May 27, 2015</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Markus" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Markus" href="/user/show/26681101-markus">Markus</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/26681101-markus?shelf=the-separatists">the-separatists</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/26681101-markus?shelf=2016">2016</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/26681101-markus?shelf=fantasy">fantasy</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1290974052" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer18436449878987676281">I have always detested Michael Moorcock.<br/><br/>As a fantasy reader whose passion for Tolkien’s writing knows no boundaries, I have always had a hard time swallowing the venomous spite and blind arrogance with which he’s been denouncing the champion of my fantasy dreams. <br/><br/>Generally speaking, I never felt anything against authors who attempted to step out of Tolkien’s shadow. These separatists, as I enjoy calling them, count shining stars like Mervyn Peake and China Mieville among their ranks, both men I </span>
  <span id="freeText18436449878987676281" style="display:none">I have always detested Michael Moorcock.<br /><br />As a fantasy reader whose passion for Tolkien’s writing knows no boundaries, I have always had a hard time swallowing the venomous spite and blind arrogance with which he’s been denouncing the champion of my fantasy dreams. <br /><br />Generally speaking, I never felt anything against authors who attempted to step out of Tolkien’s shadow. These separatists, as I enjoy calling them, count shining stars like Mervyn Peake and China Mieville among their ranks, both men I respect immensely. But with Moorcock it’s personal. Whereas Mieville countered his earlier criticism by praising Tolkien to the high heavens, Moorcock remained adamant in his contempt.<br /><br />And yet I always knew I would one day have to read his work. If for no other reason than to get to know my enemy better. But I think part of me also grudgingly admitted (subconsciously) that there has to be a certain level of awesomeness to a fantasy author writing lyrics for Hawkwind and Blue Öyster Cult.<br /><br />So I read <i>Elric of Melnibonë</i>. I confess to hoping to hate it so I could totally ravage it in a scathing review. But it was no surprise that when asked on a late night of reading how the book was, I had to reply “annoyingly good.”<br /><br />For it is good. It has many flaws, but overall I thoroughly enjoyed myself while reading it. And I can hardly wait to read more about the albino emperor of Melnibonë.<br /><br />I will probably never like Moorcock. His books, though…</span>
  <a data-text-id="18436449878987676281" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="✘✘ Sarah ✘✘ (former Nefarious Breeder of Murderous Crustaceans)" class="user" itemprop="url" name="✘✘ Sarah ✘✘ (former Nefarious Breeder of Murderous Crustaceans)" href="/user/show/7298986-sarah-former-nefarious-breeder-of-murderous-crustaceans">✘✘ Sarah ✘✘ (former Nefarious Breeder of Murderous Crustaceans)</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/7298986-sarah-former-nefarious-breeder-of-murderous-crustaceans?shelf=fantasy">fantasy</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/7298986-sarah-former-nefarious-breeder-of-murderous-crustaceans?shelf=blood-and-gore-and-violence-oh-my">blood-and-gore-and-violence-oh-my</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/7298986-sarah-former-nefarious-breeder-of-murderous-crustaceans?shelf=rr-2020">rr-2020</a>,

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2127705051" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer12071964109663716649">➽ <b><u>And the moral of this reread is</u>:</b> Elric's, um, <i>beloved</i> cousin <b>Yyrkoon</b> <s>The Gentle and Magnanimous</s> is one of the most underrated villains in the history of <b>Most Underrated Villains</b> (MUV™). Because I said so and stuff.<br/><br/><img src='https://images.gr-assets.com/hostedimages/1580321020ra/28864928.gif' class='gr-hostedUserImg'/><br/><br/>Why of course you do, my darling Yyrkie. That's why you're such a <b>scrumptiously delicious villain</b> and stuff. Now be a dear and ditch the Skeletor costume post haste, will you? You don't want to ruin your street cred, do you now? <b>That's a good boy</b>!<br/><br/><b>P.S.</b> Pigs, Snake and Thing FTW!<br/><br/><img src='https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1554139579i/27300168._SX540_.jpg' class='gr-hostedUserImg'/><br/><br/>[<b>Janua</b></span>
  <span id="freeText12071964109663716649" style="display:none">➽ <b><u>And the moral of this reread is</u>:</b> Elric's, um, <i>beloved</i> cousin <b>Yyrkoon</b> <s>The Gentle and Magnanimous</s> is one of the most underrated villains in the history of <b>Most Underrated Villains</b> (MUV™). Because I said so and stuff.<br><br><img src="https://images.gr-assets.com/hostedimages/1580321020ra/28864928.gif" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br>Why of course you do, my darling Yyrkie. That's why you're such a <b>scrumptiously delicious villain</b> and stuff. Now be a dear and ditch the Skeletor costume post haste, will you? You don't want to ruin your street cred, do you now? <b>That's a good boy</b>!<br><br><b>P.S.</b> Pigs, Snake and Thing FTW!<br><br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1554139579i/27300168._SX540_.jpg" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br>[<b>January 2019</b>]<br><br>🏰 A <b>Scarlet Citadel That Was But Is No More Sob Sob Sob Buddy Read</b> (SCTWBISMSSSBR™) with <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2657767261" rel="nofollow noopener">Elena</a> and <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2657731426" rel="nofollow noopener">Beige</a> 🏰<br><br><b>Okay, so there’s this guy called Elric</b>. (Just pointing this out in case some Clueless Barnacles are in a boozy daze and missed the title of this book and stuff.) He’s a <b>Super Hot Albino Dude</b> (SHAD™) *waves at her ex-boyfriend Geralt of R.* with <i>flesh the color of a <b>bleached skull</b></i>, milk-white long hair (because he’s, you know, an albino and stuff) and <b>very sexey red eyes</b>. (Told you he was Super Hot.) Elric descends from a long line of <s>deliciously ruthless dictators</s> well-meaning <b>Sorcerer-Emperor chaps</b> who have been <s>mercilessly ruling</s> spreading their <b>love and compassion</b> over the world in general and the island of Dragon…err…Isle in particular for ten thousand years and stuff. Oh, and by the way, there are, um, you know, <b>dragons</b> in Dragon Isle. <b>I kid you not</b>. (They were feeling a little lethargic and sleeping off their latest Mikonos escapade in this book, and therefore weren’t around much, so don’t get all excited, you dragon fangirls/fanboys/fanwhatevers.) <br><br><img src="https://images.gr-assets.com/hostedimages/1547285041ra/26905361.gif" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br><i>See what I mean?</i><br><br><b>Anyway</b>, Dragon Isle and its <s>wonderfully amoral</s> mostly benevolent leaders and stuff. Poor Elric must be the <b>unluckiest Albino Sorcerer Emperor ever</b>, because the kingdom he inherited is kinda sorta on the decline and about to get its lovely derriere kicked <b>six ways to Sunday before last</b> by the emerging nations of the Young Kingdoms (aka Puny Humans, Inc). It sucks to be him, if you ask me. Especially since he’s not exactly the picture of <b>dictatorial</b> health. He’s a pretty <b>pathetic, decrepit creature</b>, actually. (Hadn’t I known better I could have sworn he was an apathetic barnacle in disguise.) I mean, the dude is so <b>ridiculously weak and frail</b>, he has to take potions *waves at her ex-boyfriend Geralt of R. again* in order to <b>not drop deadly</b> dead and stuff. <br><br><b>Want to know another reason why it sucks to be him</b>? He isn’t what you might call a super fan of the whole <b>Sorcerer Emperor Gig Thingie</b> (SEGT™). He’d rather spend time thinking deep thoughts and reading and meditating and all that kind of silly stuff than engaging in the same <b>delightful activities</b> as his lovely subjects. You know, wonderful hobbies such as <b>rape</b>, <b>cannibalism à la carte</b>, <b>tender torture</b>, <b> “playing” with slaves</b> (no board games involved) and basically anything that involves <b>being good to thy neighbour</b>. What a sweet, pleasant bunch, these Melniboneans. <blockquote>
  <i>“Doctor Jest returned to his charges and, reaching out with his free hand, <b>expertly seized the genitals</b> of one of the male prisoners. <b>The scalpel flashed</b>. There was a groan. Doctor Jest <b>tossed something onto the fire</b>.”</i>
</blockquote><b>Ah, good old Doctor Jest</b>. The most charming of them all, assuredly. I’m this close to giving <a href="https://i.postimg.cc/zXvMfv8c/ycKJs4F.gif" rel="nofollow noopener">Doctor Prawn</a> the boot, and <s>kidnapping</s> temporarily borrowing Jest-the-Hilariously-Named to replace him and stuff. But I digress. So Elric, being the <b>scholarly bore</b> that he is, doesn’t really approve of his peons’ slightly violent, <b>unethical way of life</b>. And his peons, being the moderately unbalanced, homicidal clique that they are, aren’t huge fans of their not-so-beloved Emperor’s despicably principled, <b>philosophizing ways</b>. And that’s where <b>Prince Yyrkoon</b> comes in. He is Elric’s cousin, and next in the line of succession, you see. And <b>worries a whole lot</b> about his beloved relative’s well-being. Of course he does. He’s a kind, loving soul, and completely devoid of <b>personal ambition</b>. The last thing he wants is to kill Elric deadly dead and/or take his place. <b>Obviously</b>.<br><br><img src="https://images.gr-assets.com/hostedimages/1547289543ra/26905624.gif" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br><i>Such a comedian, that Yyrkoon.</i><br><br><b>What happens next</b>? Well spoiler spoiler spoiler, obviously. And also <b>Slightly Yummy Sea Battles</b>™ (arrr!!!), elemental gods you don’t want to make deals with (oops, too late), <b>severed heads</b> (yay!), Barbarians (no <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/817829.The_Coming_of_Conan_the_Cimmerian" rel="nofollow noopener">Cimmerian Sweetie Pie</a> in sight, unfortunately), the <i>“munificence of fear”</i>, <b>good jokes</b> (or bad jokes, depending on one’s perspective), supremely villainous, comic book-type, <s>worms</s> <b>villainous villains</b>, sorcerous stuff (duh), <b>groaning mists</b>, a ship so <b>bloody shrimping cool </b> I’m putting it on my Nefarious Christmas Wishlist, <b>magic mirrors</b> (the Evil Queen should have trademarked them when she had the chance, methinks), thwarted plans (aka fun times), a couple of <b>stubborn, sentient swords</b>, sweet sardonic voices (♫music to my ears♫), <b>baby sacrifices</b> (finally, someone who understands me!), <b>
  <i>Pulsing Caverns</i>
</b> (which apparently remind some, um, eccentric people I shall not name *waves at Elena* of a, um, certain female body part), bestial (if a little <i>coarse and stupid</i>) demons, <b>Pig and Snake and Thing</b> (oh my!), and last, but certainly not least, <b>eye-less, vomit-adorned, brain-bashed, self-mutilated corpses</b>. YUM.<br><br><img src="https://images.gr-assets.com/hostedimages/1547291856ra/26905852.gif" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br>➽ <b>Nefarious Last Words</b> (NLW™): the crustaceans and my little self find it <b>stupendously hilarious</b> that Moorcock intended for Elric’s adventures to be the <b>antithesis</b> of my Barbarian Paramour’s, because some of the stuff in this book is just as <b>lusciously clichéd</b> as <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/66700.Robert_E__Howard" title="Robert E. Howard" rel="nofollow noopener">Robert E. Howard</a>’s most OTT stories. <b>Which is pretty fishing awesome, if you ask me</b>.<br><br>· <b>Book 1</b>: <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2127705051" rel="nofollow noopener">Elric of Melniboné</a> ★★★★★<br>· <b>Book 2</b>: <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2692745184" rel="nofollow noopener">The Fortress of the Pearl</a> ★★★★★<br>· <b>Book 3</b>: <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/3122489015" rel="nofollow noopener">The Sailor on the Seas of Fate</a> ★★★★★<br>· <b>Book 4</b>: <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/3233478823" rel="nofollow noopener">The Weird of the White Wolf</a> ★★★<br>· <b>Book 5</b>: <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/3234560733" rel="nofollow noopener">The Vanishing Tower</a> (aka “The Sleeping Sorceress”) - <i>to be read</i>.<br>· <b>Book 6</b>: <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/3234570986" rel="nofollow noopener">The Revenge of the Black Rose</a> - <i>to be read</i>.<br>· <b>Book 7</b>: <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/3234577453" rel="nofollow noopener">The Bane of the Black Sword</a> - <i>to be read</i>.<br>· <b>Book 8</b>: <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/3234579370" rel="nofollow noopener">Stormbringer</a> - <i>to be read</i>.<br>· <b>Book 9</b>: <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/3234589329" rel="nofollow noopener">Elric at the End of Time</a> - <i>to be read</i>.<br>· <b>Book 10</b>: <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/3234594839" rel="nofollow noopener">Daughter of Dreams</a> - <i>to be read</i>.<br>· <b>Book 11</b>: <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/3234597309" rel="nofollow noopener">Destiny’s Brother</a> - <i>to be read</i>.<br>· <b>Book 12</b>: <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/3234600673" rel="nofollow noopener">Son of the Wolf</a> - <i>to be read</i>.<br><br>(Following the <a href="https://www.tor.com/tag/the-elric-reread/" rel="nofollow noopener">Tor reading order</a>)<br><br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1479540926i/21203689._SX540_.jpg" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br>[<b>Pre-review nonsense</b>]<br><br>An <b>albino MC</b> with flesh the color of a <b>bleached skull</b> and slanting, <b>crimson eyes</b>? Yummy.<br><br><img src="https://images.gr-assets.com/hostedimages/1546670951ra/26861699.gif" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br><b>By the way</b>, young Elric, do you not happen to have a third cousin thrice removed called <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/40603587-the-last-wish" rel="nofollow noopener">Geralt of R.</a>? He was one of my High Security Harem <s>slaves</s> boarders for a while (we kinda sorta had a <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/14781491-the-time-of-contempt" rel="nofollow noopener">very minor falling-out</a> and I kinda sorta banned him for a somewhat permanent while), and you remind me <b>slightly very much</b> of him and stuff, so I thought you might be related and stuff.<br><br>➽ Full <b>There Ain’t Naught Like Classic Sword &amp; Sorcery Tales Methinks Crappy Non-Review</b> (TANLCSSTMCNR™) to come.</span>

<div id="review_1865799405" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1865799405" />
    <a title="Bill Kerwin" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/83582-bill-kerwin"><img alt="Bill Kerwin" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1442940975p2/83582.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1865799405?book_show_action=true">Jan 19, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Bill Kerwin" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Bill Kerwin" href="/user/show/83582-bill-kerwin">Bill Kerwin</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/83582-bill-kerwin?shelf=fantasy">fantasy</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/83582-bill-kerwin?shelf=fiction">fiction</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1865799405" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer113617228988194340"><br/>It pleases me to return, after many years, to the saga of Elric VIII, Emperor of Melnibone, the doomed albino with the icy demeanor and imperious nature whose constant companion is Stormbringer, a bloodthirsty sword with a consciousness and will all its own. <br/><br/>Elric is the sickly descendant of a haughty alien race, a long line of amoral conquerors and wielders of magic. Many of his nobles—including his cousin, the accomplished magician Yrkoon—do not think the young emperor is fit for his high task</span>
  <span id="freeText113617228988194340" style="display:none"><br />It pleases me to return, after many years, to the saga of Elric VIII, Emperor of Melnibone, the doomed albino with the icy demeanor and imperious nature whose constant companion is Stormbringer, a bloodthirsty sword with a consciousness and will all its own. <br /><br />Elric is the sickly descendant of a haughty alien race, a long line of amoral conquerors and wielders of magic. Many of his nobles—including his cousin, the accomplished magician Yrkoon—do not think the young emperor is fit for his high task. He is soon challenged by a pirate fleet from The Young Kingdoms, and the threats that that arise from this challenge soon endanger not only the princess Cymoril but the throne of the Dragon Isle itself.<br /><br />This is an excellent beginning to a first rate series, a series which showed me fantasy could offer alien and uncomfortable beauties, hard pleasures far removed from the comforts of Professor Tolkien’s snug shire.</span>

<div id="review_570806024" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/570806024" />
    <a title="Bradley" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/4213258-bradley"><img alt="Bradley" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1508811508p2/4213258.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/570806024?book_show_action=true">Mar 25, 2013</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Bradley" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Bradley" href="/user/show/4213258-bradley">Bradley</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4213258-bradley?shelf=fantasy">fantasy</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4213258-bradley?shelf=2016-shelf">2016-shelf</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4213258-bradley?shelf=fanboy-goes-squee">fanboy-goes-squee</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer570806024" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer4858677992889029201">This is some epic awesomeness.<br/><br/>I'm an absolute sucker for the grand sweeping personal quests to gain more and yet more magical power in the service of rescuing your one true love, casting aside morals, the greater good, your own health, and possibly your own sanity. <br/><br/>This tale holds up perfectly after all this time. All the best aspects of modern fantasy are encapsulated and written with such spartan clarity and diamond sharpness within Moorcock's classic. I only needed this one taste and I'm now </span>
  <span id="freeText4858677992889029201" style="display:none">This is some epic awesomeness.<br /><br />I'm an absolute sucker for the grand sweeping personal quests to gain more and yet more magical power in the service of rescuing your one true love, casting aside morals, the greater good, your own health, and possibly your own sanity. <br /><br />This tale holds up perfectly after all this time. All the best aspects of modern fantasy are encapsulated and written with such spartan clarity and diamond sharpness within Moorcock's classic. I only needed this one taste and I'm now a lifelong fan. It's that easy. <br /><br />This is easily one of the classics of all Sword and Sorcery and I knew that people swore by it before I read it, but I was hesitant. Why? Hell if I know. It was such a brilliant, fantastic, imaginative world, but even better than that were the characters. Elric, of course, is the ultimate Nietzsche Super-Man, fully beyond good and evil, but he, like all super-villains, considers himself the ultimate hero of his story, and I have to agree with him. I love the story.<br /><br />It's really cool that I've finally read something, after all this time, that evokes the same feeling as I get from the classic Star Wars films. When they faced each other with the two uber-powerful runic swords, I got chills. Seriously. I usually don't get suckered in this easy. I've read a LOT of fantasy and a LOT of really great fantasy, but this one was so diamond-hard that it left me speechless. :)<br /><br />I was even more impressed by the author's command of sheer storytelling. The whole thing actually evoked awe and wonder. Each new mastery of magic came at a cost, and there was so much magic. Like the Ouroboros, Elric kept feeding on himself to gain more and more power, and the cycle repeated and repeated, with future sacrifice for power, now. Is it the ultimate faustian tale? I don't know, yet, but I'll be reading more. As it is here, we've got a meteoric rise from the simple mastery of the kingdom of Melniboné to the mastery of chaos magic, the elementals, and the godlike black sword. <br /><br />Do you want a character so awfully OP that nothing, absolutely nothing, can stand in his way? Hell yeah. I'm a gamer. Do you want to have a story that manages to take him and never make him boring? Hell yeah! Here you go!<br /><br />I'm seriously ashamed that I never got into this earlier. I knew it was out there. I know the author is recognized as one of the greats of fantasy. And now I know why, and I'm hooked. :)</span>
  <a data-text-id="4858677992889029201" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <div class="friendReviews elementListBrown" >
    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_1254036805" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1254036805" />
    <a title="Algernon (Darth Anyan)" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/4728718-algernon-darth-anyan"><img alt="Algernon (Darth Anyan)" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1553101944p2/4728718.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1254036805?book_show_action=true">Apr 13, 2015</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Algernon (Darth Anyan)" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Algernon (Darth Anyan)" href="/user/show/4728718-algernon-darth-anyan">Algernon (Darth Anyan)</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780879973568" href="/book/show/1026508.Elric_of_Melnibon_">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4728718-algernon-darth-anyan?shelf=2015">2015</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1254036805" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer3494957382024879878"><br/><img src='https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1429718651i/14623643.jpg' width='400' alt='cover' class='gr-hostedUserImg'/><br/><br/><i> This is the tale of Elric before he was called Woman-slayer, before the final collapse of Melnibone. This is the tale of his rivalry with his cousin Yirkoon and his love for his cousin Cymoril, before that rivalry and that love brought Imrryr, the Dreaming City, crashing in flames, raped by the reavers from the Young Kingdoms. This is the tale of the two black swords, Stormbringer and Mournblade, and how they were discovered and what part they played in the destiny of Elric and Melnibone - a de</i></span>
  <span id="freeText3494957382024879878" style="display:none"><br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1429718651i/14623643.jpg" width="400" alt="cover" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br><i> This is the tale of Elric before he was called Woman-slayer, before the final collapse of Melnibone. This is the tale of his rivalry with his cousin Yirkoon and his love for his cousin Cymoril, before that rivalry and that love brought Imrryr, the Dreaming City, crashing in flames, raped by the reavers from the Young Kingdoms. This is the tale of the two black swords, Stormbringer and Mournblade, and how they were discovered and what part they played in the destiny of Elric and Melnibone - a destiny which was to shape a larger destiny: that of the world itself. This is the tale of when Elric was a king, the commander of dragons, fleets and all the folk of that half-human race which had ruled the world for ten thousand years.<br>This is a tale of tragedy, this tale of Melnibone, the Dragon Isle. This is a tale of monstrous emotions and high ambitions. This is a tale of sorceries and treacheries and worthy ideals, of agonies and fearful pleasures, of bitter love and sweet hatred. This is the tale of Elric of Melnibone. Much of it Elric himself was to remember only in his nightmares. </i><br><br>Elric should sit proudly in a fantasy Hall of Fame. In terms of influence on the development of the genre, Elric has few rivals. Before the rise of the moody albino swordman-sorcerer, there was basically a choice between the brutality of Conan and the knightly scions of Tolkien. In the footsteps of Moorcock I have noticed an increasing preference for anti-heroes, for more nuanced characterization, for 50+ shades of grey. He is reluctant to assume the power he inherited, introspective and plagued by ethical doubts. Even physically, he is sickly, needing daily potions to regenerate his strength. Elric turns his weakness into an advantage by becoming bookish, reading entire libraries on magic, one more reason to consider him atypical of the staple fantasy stable boy with a secret identity. <br><br><i> If the young emperor has found any advantage in his lifelong weakness it must be in that, perforce, he has read much. </i><br><br>One aspect in particular makes me count the Elric saga a watershed moment in fantasy, a splitting of the mainstream in two directions: for Tolkien and his imitators the gods or Fate rule the destiny of the mortals. Elric rebels against prophecy and predestination, and argues for the supremacy of free-will and chance.<br><br><b> The lords of the Higher Worlds have ambitions in our world. Though they have given me aid, of late, I fear them. I should like to see if it is possible for men to rule their own affairs. </b><br><br>I should include in the list of anti-heroes other sword &amp; sorcery founders, like Fritz Leiber or Jack Vance, but this would complicate my theoretical argument of the divided stream. And I don't want to focus too much in my review on generalities, because the appeal of the first Elric book is that in a couple of hundred pages he manages to build an epic scope to the story, to infuse it with a sense of wonder and adventure, to mix magic and gods with martial combat and human passion - love, greed, doubt, pride, friendship, hate. <br><br>The epic begins by introducing the reader to the young emperor of 'Imrryr the Beautiful, the Dreaming City, capital of the Dragon Isle of Melnibone.', our future guide through the histories, races and sights of a hundred worlds or more. It may not be apparent in this first novel, but in the universe imagined by Moorcock time and space are flexible, full of portals to parallel realities and alternative timelines. Elric is like The Hero with a Thousand Faces - an avatar, a catalyst of change, a mythical figure that breaks and reconfigures what is real. He is also unique and memorable in his physical appearance:<br><br><i>It is the colour of a bleached skull, his flesh; and the long hair which flows below his shoulders is milk-white. From the tapering, beautiful head stare two slanting eyes, crimson and moody, and from the loose sleeves of his yellow gown emerge two slender hands, also the colour of bone, resting on each arm of a seat which has been carved from a single, massive ruby. </i><br><br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1429718652i/14623644._SY540_.jpg" width="400" alt="brom" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br>For a synopsis, my opening quote from the fictional chronicles of his life should suffice. It is also illustrative of the style of writing - a touch too florid at times but clearly the work of a young writer enthusiastic about the genre. I have read a few years ago a book that Moorcock cites as an important influence on Elric: "The Broken Sword" by Poul Anderson. There are indeed similarities in the language used and in the type of hero who refuses to accept predestination. Moorcock I believe is better at capturing the sense of wonder and discovery, as in the description of the 'Ship Which Sails Over Land and Sea' :<br><br><i> The ship was tall and slender and she was delicate. Her rails, masts and bulwarks were exquisitely carved and obviously not the work of a mortal craftsman. Though built of wood, the wood was not painted but naturally shone blue and black and green and a kind of deep smoky red, and her rigging was the colour of sea-weed and there were veins in the planks of her polished deck, like the roots of trees, and the sails of her three tapering masts were as fat and white and light as clouds on a fine summer day. The ship was everything that was lovely in nature; few could look upon her and not feel delighted upon sighting a perfect view. </i><br><br>Another defining characteristic of the Elric saga is the downbeat tone, the sense of an ending of an era: the last dragons are sleeping, the most beautiful city in the world will become a ruin, young lovers are separated and tortured, Elric himself is prone to depression and despair:<br><br><i> Rackhir laughed and slapped the albino upon his black armoured back. "You are a gloomy comrade, friend Elric. Are all your thoughts so hopeless?"<br>"They tend in that direction, I fear," said Elric with a shadow of a smile. </i><br><br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1429718652i/14623645._SY540_.jpg" width="400" alt="doom" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br>Don't expect jolly hobbits and lovable scoundrels. The Melniboneans are an old race and jaded to pleasure or ambition, only half-human, closer to Tolkien's elves in their inception, on their way out from the world scene. Elric is the only one who shows signs of a conscience and of curiosity, and these traits will make an outcast of him. Other possible limitations of the series:<br>- it is addressed mainly to fans of sword &amp; sorcery, so it's appeal outside the genre is limited<br>- the secondary characters are under-developed<br>- the style is not as polished and subtle as, for example, in the works of Jack Vance or Karl Edward Wagner. There is a lot of promise and beautiful passages, but this first novel is clearly an early effort.<br>- it could benefit from a little more humour and less sadness<br>- it might feel dated as a seventies story, with some drug experimentation and symbolism along the lines of : "Enter the Shade Gate, then you must seek the Tunnel under the Marsh which leads to the Pulsing Cavern. In that chamber the runeswords are kept." The sexual analogy of the womb and rebirth are clear enough.<br><br><br>All in all, a masterpiece of fantasy, but with limited interest for the general public. I look forward to reading the rest of the Elric stories. As a last teaser, Moorcock has this to say about what lays in store for his albino wanderer:<br><br><b> Elric's destiny has been forged and fixed as surely as the hellswords were forged and fixed aeons before. Was there ever a point where he might have turned off this road to despair, damnation and destruction? Or has he been doomed since before his birth? Doomed through a thousand incarnations to know little else but sadness and struggle, loneliness and remorse - eternally the champion of some unknown cause? </b><br></span>
  <a data-text-id="3494957382024879878" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_2639831723" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2639831723" />
    <a title="Karl" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1935255-karl"><img alt="Karl" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1588969235p2/1935255.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2639831723?book_show_action=true">Dec 26, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Karl" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Karl" href="/user/show/1935255-karl">Karl</a>
      </span>

          added it


          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Hardcover " href="/book/show/43365845-elric-of-melnibon">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1935255-karl?shelf=centipede-milipede-own">centipede-milipede-own</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1935255-karl?shelf=2018-12-books-bought">2018-12-books-bought</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">

<div id="review_463799756" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/463799756" />
    <a title="Dirk Grobbelaar" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/3102740-dirk-grobbelaar"><img alt="Dirk Grobbelaar" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1545117991p2/3102740.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/463799756?book_show_action=true">Jan 15, 2020</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Dirk Grobbelaar" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Dirk Grobbelaar" href="/user/show/3102740-dirk-grobbelaar">Dirk Grobbelaar</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Mass Market Paperback 9780586203262" href="/book/show/445211.Elric_of_Melnibon_">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3102740-dirk-grobbelaar?shelf=books-i-own">books-i-own</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3102740-dirk-grobbelaar?shelf=acquired-in-2013">acquired-in-2013</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3102740-dirk-grobbelaar?shelf=fantasy">fantasy</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3102740-dirk-grobbelaar?shelf=actually-read-on-an-island">actually-read-on-an-island</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer463799756" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer3176749980394111751"><i>It is the colour of a bleached skull, his flesh; and the long hair which flows below his shoulders is milk-white.</i><br/><br/>Here is a funny piece of random information: my first introduction to the doomed albino and his demonic sword was not through literature but through music, namely the songs “Black Blade” (Blue Oyster Cult) and “Song Of The Swords” (Hawkwind). As someone who has always enjoyed pulp fiction and old school fantasy, I was very aware of Elric of Melniboné. He is, after all, one of the more</span>
  <span id="freeText3176749980394111751" style="display:none"><i>It is the colour of a bleached skull, his flesh; and the long hair which flows below his shoulders is milk-white.</i><br /><br />Here is a funny piece of random information: my first introduction to the doomed albino and his demonic sword was not through literature but through music, namely the songs “Black Blade” (Blue Oyster Cult) and “Song Of The Swords” (Hawkwind). As someone who has always enjoyed pulp fiction and old school fantasy, I was very aware of Elric of Melniboné. He is, after all, one of the more iconic of the bunch, so it is a mystery why I only got around to reading any Elric fiction until so late in the game. <br /><br />But: better late than never. <br /><br /><i>When Elric arrived back at his apartments it was to discover that Tanglebones had already laid out his heavy, black wargear. Here was the armour which had served a hundred Melnibonean emperors; an armour which was forged by sorcery to give it a strength unequalled on the Realm of Earth, which could, so rumour went, even withstand the bite of the mythical runeblades, Stormbringer and Mournblade, which had been wielded by the wickedest of Melnibone's many wicked rulers before being seized by the Lords of the Higher Worlds and hidden forever in a realm where even those Lords might rarely venture.</i><br /><br />It is easy to see that Elric has, to some extent or other, influenced many other fantasy characters. Off the top of my head I’m thinking of Geralt of Rivia (<a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/40603587.The_Last_Wish__The_Witcher___0_5_" title="The Last Wish (The Witcher, #0.5) by Andrzej Sapkowski" rel="nofollow noopener">The Last Wish</a>) and Malus Darkblade (<a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/33622622.The_Daemon_s_Curse__The_Chronicles_of_Malus_Darkblade_Book_1_" title="The Daemon's Curse (The Chronicles of Malus Darkblade Book 1) by Dan Abnett" rel="nofollow noopener">The Daemon's Curse</a>). I’m specifically saying “to some extent” because neither of the latter are exact copies, but there are definitely some overlapping traits that appear to be borrowed from Moorcock’s iconic anti-hero.<br /><br />But back to the book at hand. It was a fantastic read, to be sure. I’m not giving it five stars because the latter half of the novel had some elements that I found <i>juuust</i> a bit hokey. No deal breaker, but, for example the meeting with the Earth King Grome wasn’t nearly as cool as the meeting with the Sea King Straasha earlier in the book, and the battle with Pig, Snake and Thing was just a bit of an acid trip. More importantly though: I wasn’t quite sold on the Mirror of Memory, which is actually quite a huge plot device, explaining how Elric hadn’t been able to locate Yyrkoon. Anyway, these are small gripes and I’m going into detail that is irrelevant to the review. In the end it was still a fantastic read and I highly recommend it to anybody who hasn’t read Elric yet. Especially if you enjoy the older stuff, but with a bit of a dark bent. I do think, however, that I should read the short stories next, before moving on to the next novel; Elric seems like a character that would translate extremely well to the short form.<br /><br /><i>And in the centre of the cavern, hanging in the air without any support at all were two swords. Two identical swords, huge and fine and black.</i> </span>
  <a data-text-id="3176749980394111751" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>
